PRs that are\n    50‚Üícherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\n    51‚Üíto the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\n    52‚Üíchanging the patch version.\n    53‚Üí\n    54‚Üí#### Branches and tags.\n    55‚Üí\n    56‚ÜíWe will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\n    57‚Üínumber. 62‚Üíbranch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n    63‚Üí\n    64‚Üí#### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n    65‚Üí\n    66‚ÜíThe `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\n    67‚Üídifferent versions.\n    68‚Üí\n    69‚ÜíSee [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\n    70‚Üíversion of client-go.\n    71‚Üí\n    72‚Üí#### Compatibility: From Kubernetes `v1.17.0`, we also create matching semver\n   153‚Üí`v0.x.y` tags for each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release.\n   154‚Üí\n   155‚ÜíFor example, if you check out the `kubernetes-1.17.0` or the `v0.17.0` tag in\n   156‚Üíclient-go, the code you get is exactly the same as if you check out the `v1.17.0`\n   157‚Üítag in Kubernetes, and change directory to `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go`.\n   158‚Üí\n   159‚ÜíThe purpose is to let users quickly find matching commits among published repos,\n   160‚Üílike [sample-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-apiserver),\n   161‚Üí[apiextension-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/apiextensions-apiserver),\n   162‚Üíetc. The Kubernetes version tag does NOT claim any backwards compatibility\n   163‚Üíguarantees for client-go. Please check the [semantic versions](#versioning) if\n   164‚Üíyou care about backwards compatibility.\n   165‚Üí\n   166‚Üí### How to get it\n   167‚Üí\n   168‚ÜíTo get the latest version, use go1.16+ and fetch using the `go get` command. [Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?](#why-do-the-14-and-15-branch-contain-top-level-folder-named-after-the-version)\n- [Kubernetes tags](#kubernetes-tags)\n- [How to get it](#how-to-get-it)\n- [How to use it](#how-to-use-it)\n- [Dependency management](#dependency-management)\n- [Contributing code](#contributing-code)\n\n### What's included\n\n* The `kubernetes` package contains the clientset to access Kubernetes API.\n* The `discovery` package is used to discover APIs supported by a Kubernetes API server.\n* The `dynamic` package contains a dynamic client that can perform generic operations on arbitrary Kubernetes API objects.\n* The `plugin/pkg/client/auth` packages contain optional authentication plugins for obtaining credentials from external sources.\n* The `transport` package is used to set up auth and start a connection.\n* The `tools/cache` package is useful for writing controllers.\n\n### Versioning\n\n- For each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release, the major version (first digit)\nwould remain `0`.\n\n- Bugfixes will result in the patch version (third digit) changing. PRs that are\ncherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\nto the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\nchanging the patch version.\n\n#### Branches and tags.\n\nWe will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\nnumber. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\nversion number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\nminor, and patch.\n\nThe HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\nbranch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n\n#### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n\nThe `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\ndifferent versions.\n\nSee [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\nversion of client-go.\n\n#### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\n\nSince Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\nversions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\n\nWe will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions of\n`client-go`.\n\n\n#### Compatibility matrix\n\n|                               | Kubernetes 1.29 | Kubernetes 1.30 | Kubernetes 1.31 | Kubernetes 1.32 | Kubernetes 1.33 | Kubernetes 1.34 |\n| ----------------------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- |\n| `kubernetes-1.29.0`/`v0.29.0` | ‚úì               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.30.0`/`v0.30.0` | +-              | ‚úì               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.31.0`/`v0.31.0` | +-              | +-              | ‚úì               | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n| `kubernetes-1.32.0`/`v0.32.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | ‚úì               | `kubernetes-1.34.0`/`v0.34.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ‚úì               |\n| `HEAD`                        | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\n\nKey:\n\n* `‚úì` Exactly the same features / API objects in both client-go and the Kubernetes\n  version.\n* `+` client-go has features or API objects that may not be present in the\n  Kubernetes cluster, either due to that client-go has additional new API, or\n  that the server has removed old API. However, everything they have in\n  common (i.e., most APIs) will work. Please note that alpha APIs may vanish or\n  change significantly in a single release.\n* `-` The Kubernetes cluster has features the client-go library can't use,\n  either due to the server has additional new API, or that client-go has\n  removed old API. However, everything they share in common (i.e., most APIs)\n  will work.\n\nSee the [CHANGELOG](./CHANGELOG.md) for a detailed description of changes\nbetween client-go versions.\n\n| Branch         | Canonical source code location      | Maintenance status |\n| -------------- | ----------------------------------- | ------------------ |\n| `release-1.25` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.25 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.26` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.26 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.27` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.27 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.28` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.28 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.29` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.29 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.30` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.30 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.31` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.31 branch   | =                  |\n| `release-1.32` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.32 branch   | ‚úì                  |\n| `release-1.33` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.33 branch   | ‚úì                  |\n| `release-1.34` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.34 branch   | ‚úì                  |\n| client-go HEAD | Kubernetes main repo, master branch | ‚úì                  |\n\nKey:\n\n* `‚úì` Changes in main Kubernetes repo are actively published to client-go by a bot\n* `=` Maintenance is manual, only severe security bugs will be patched.\n* `-` Deprecated; please upgrade.\n\n#### Deprecation policy\n\nWe will maintain branches for at least six months after their first stable tag\nis cut. (E.g., the clock for the release-2.0 branch started ticking when we\ntagged v2.0.0, not when we made the first alpha.) This policy applies to\nevery version greater than or equal to 2.0.\n\n#### Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?\n\nFor the initial release of client-go, we thought it would be easiest to keep\nseparate directories for each minor version. From Kubernetes `v1.17.0`, we also create matching semver\n`v0.x.y` tags for each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release.\n\nFor example, if you check out the `kubernetes-1.17.0` or the `v0.17.0` tag in\nclient-go, the code you get is exactly the same as if you check out the `v1.17.0`\ntag in Kubernetes, and change directory to `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go`.\n\nThe purpose is to let users quickly find matching commits among published repos,\nlike [sample-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-apiserver),\n[apiextension-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/apiextensions-apiserver),\netc. The Kubernetes version tag does NOT claim any backwards compatibility\nguarantees for client-go. It describes the\n     4‚Üímajor components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n     5‚Üí\n     6‚Üí## Client Configuration\n     7‚Üí\n     8‚ÜíThere is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n     9‚Üí`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n    10‚Üí`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n    11‚Üícomplex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n    12‚Üíauthentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n    13‚Üí\n    14‚Üí## REST Client\n    15‚Üí\n    16‚ÜíThe `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n    17‚Üíthe low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n    18‚ÜíKubernetes-specific object logic.\n    19‚Üí\n    20‚ÜíThe `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n    21‚Üíchain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n    22‚Üítask, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n    23‚Üíis injected into requests.\n    24‚Üí\n    25‚ÜíThe client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n    26‚Üíresponse processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n    27‚Üísupporting different client models from a common base.\n    28‚Üí\n    29‚Üí### Endpoint Interactions\n    30‚Üí\n    31‚Üí*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    32‚Üí    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    33‚Üí    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    34‚Üí    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n    35‚Üí*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    36‚Üí    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    37‚Üí    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n    38‚Üí*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    39‚Üí    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    40‚Üí    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    41‚Üí    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n    42‚Üí*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    43‚Üí    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    44‚Üí    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    45‚Üí    the underlying streaming protocol.\n    46‚Üí\n    47‚Üí### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n    48‚Üí\n    49‚Üí*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    50‚Üí    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n    51‚Üí*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    52‚Üí    `WarningHandler`.\n    53‚Üí*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    54‚Üí    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n    55‚Üí*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    56‚Üí    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n    57‚Üí\n    58‚Üí## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n    59‚Üí\n    60‚ÜíTo handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n    61‚Üímodels.\n    62‚Üí\n    63‚ÜíThe **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n    64‚Üí\n    65‚ÜíThe **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n    66‚Üíto interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n    67‚Üí1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    68‚Üí    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n    69‚Üí2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    70‚Üí    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n    71‚Üí\n    72‚Üí## Code Generation\n    73‚Üí\n    74‚ÜíA core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n    75‚Üístrongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n    76‚Üícontroller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n    77‚Üíseveral key components:\n    78‚Üí\n    79‚Üí*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n    80‚Üí*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n    81‚Üí*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n    82‚Üí*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n    83‚Üí\n    84‚ÜíA contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n    85‚Üíof these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n    86‚Üí\n    87‚Üí`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n    88‚Üí\n    89‚Üí## Controller Infrastructure\n    90‚Üí\n    91‚ÜíThe `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n    92‚Üírequest-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n    93‚Üí\n    94‚ÜíThe data flow is as follows:\n    95‚Üí\n    96‚Üí```mermaid\n    97‚Üígraph TD\n    98‚Üí    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n    99‚Üí        API_Server[API Server]\n   100‚Üí    end\n   101‚Üí\n   102‚Üí    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n   103‚Üí        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n   104‚Üí        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n   105‚Üí        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n   106‚Üí        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n   107‚Üí    end\n   108‚Üí\n   109‚Üí    subgraph \"User Code\"\n   110‚Üí        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n   111‚Üí        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n   112‚Üí    end\n   113‚Üí\n   114‚Üí    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n   115‚Üí    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n   116‚Üí    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n   117‚Üí    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n   118‚Üí    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n   119‚Üí    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n   120‚Üí    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n   121‚Üí```\n   122‚Üí\n   123‚ÜíA **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n   124‚Üí`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n   125‚Üístream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n   126‚Üí**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n   127‚Üí`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n   128‚Üí`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n   129‚Üí\n   130‚ÜíThe **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n   131‚Üílogic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n   132‚Üí\n   133‚Üí## Controller Patterns\n   134‚Üí\n   135‚ÜíThe controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n   136‚Üíensure resiliency.\n   137‚Üí\n   138‚ÜíThe **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n   139‚Üíand reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n   140‚Üíwork queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n   141‚Üíblocking the informer's watch stream.\n   142‚Üí\n   143‚ÜíFor high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n   144‚Üísolution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n   145‚Üíshared `Lease` object.\n   146‚Üí\n   147‚Üí## Server-Side Apply\n   148‚Üí\n   149‚Üí`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n   150‚Üíserver's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n   151‚Üímodel that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n   152‚Üí**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n   153‚Üíconstruct the declarative patch.\n   154‚Üí\n   155‚Üí## Versioning and Compatibility\n   156‚Üí\n   157‚Üí`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n   158‚Üíversion `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n   159‚Üí\n   160‚ÜíThe Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n   161‚Üíversion of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n   162‚ÜíA contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n   163‚Üí\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. It describes the\nmajor components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n\n## Client Configuration\n\nThere is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\ncomplex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\nauthentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n\n## REST Client\n\nThe `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\nthe low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\nKubernetes-specific object logic.\n\nThe `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\nchain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\ntask, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\nis injected into requests.\n\nThe client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\nresponse processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\nsupporting different client models from a common base.\n\n### Endpoint Interactions\n\n*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    the underlying streaming protocol.\n\n### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n\n*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    `WarningHandler`.\n*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n\n## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n\nTo handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\nmodels.\n\nThe **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n\nThe **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\nto interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n\n## Code Generation\n\nA core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\nstrongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\ncontroller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\nseveral key components:\n\n*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n\nA contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\nof these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n\n`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n\n## Controller Infrastructure\n\nThe `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\nrequest-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n\nThe data flow is as follows:\n\n```mermaid\ngraph TD\n    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n        API_Server[API Server]\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"User Code\"\n        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n    end\n\n    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n```\n\nA **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\nstream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n\nThe **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\nlogic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n\n## Controller Patterns\n\nThe controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\nensure resiliency.\n\nThe **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\nand reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\nwork queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\nblocking the informer's watch stream.\n\nFor high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\nsolution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\nshared `Lease` object.\n\n## Server-Side Apply\n\n`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\nserver's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\nmodel that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\nconstruct the declarative patch.\n\n## Versioning and Compatibility\n\n`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\nversion `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n\nThe Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\nversion of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\nA contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n","numLines":163,"startLine":1,"totalLines":163}}}

efficient and may lead to serious performance and\n\t// scalability problems.\n\tWatchListPageSize int64\n\t// ShouldResync is invoked periodically and whenever it returns `true` the Store's Resync operation is invoked\n\tShouldResync func() bool\n\t// MaxInternalErrorRetryDuration defines how long we should retry internal errors returned by watch.\n\tMaxInternalErrorRetryDuration time.Duration\n\t// useWatchList if turned on instructs the reflector to open a stream to bring data from the API server.\n\t// Streaming has the primary advantage of using fewer server's resources to fetch data.\n\t//\n\t// The old behaviour establishes a LIST request which gets data in chunks.\n\t// Paginated list is less efficient and depending on the actual size of objects\n\t// might result in an increased memory consumption of the APIServer.\n\t//\n\t// See https://github.com/kubernetes/enhancements/tree/master/keps/sig-api-machinery/3157-watch-list#design-details\n\tuseWatchList bool\n}\n\nfunc (r *Reflector) Name() string {\n\treturn r.name\n}\n\nfunc (r *Reflector) TypeDescription() string {\n\treturn r.typeDescription\n}\n\n// ResourceVersionUpdater is an interface that allows store implementation to\n// track the current resource version of the reflector. This is especially\n// important if storage bookmarks are enabled.\ntype ResourceVersionUpdater interface {\n\t// UpdateResourceVersion is called each time current resource version of the reflector\n\t// is updated.\n\tUpdateResourceVersion(resourceVersion string)\n}\n\n// The WatchErrorHandler is called whenever ListAndWatch drops the\n// connection with an error. After calling this handler, the informer\n// will backoff and retry.\n//\n// The default implementation looks at the error type and tries to log\n// the error message at an appropriate level.\n//\n// Implementations of this handler may display the error message in other\n// ways. Implementations should return quickly - any expensive processing\n// should be offloaded.\ntype WatchErrorHandler func(r *Reflector, err error)\n\n// The WatchErrorHandler is called whenever ListAndWatch drops the\n// connection with an error. After calling this handler, the informer\n// will backoff and retry.\n//\n// The default implementation looks at the error type and tries to log\n// the error message at an appropriate level.\n//\n// Implementations of this handler may display the error message in other\n// ways. Implementations should return quickly - any expensive processing\n// should be offloaded.\ntype WatchErrorHandlerWithContext func(ctx context.Context, r *Reflector, err error)\n\n// DefaultWatchErrorHandler is the default implementation of WatchErrorHandlerWithContext.\nfunc DefaultWatchErrorHandler(ctx context.Context, r *Reflector, err error) {\n\tswitch {\n\tcase isExpiredError(err):\n\t\t// Don't set LastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable - LIST call with ResourceVersion=RV already\n\t\t// has a semantic that it returns data at least as fresh as provided RV.\n\t\t// So first try to LIST with setting RV to resource version of last observed object.\n\t\tklog.FromContext(ctx).V(4).Info(\"Watch closed\", \"reflector\", r.name, \"type\", r.typeDescription, \"err\", err)\n\tcase err == io.EOF:\n\t\t// watch closed normally","numLines":200,"startLine":1,"totalLines":1253}}}

One is that the\n// accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n// but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n// object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n// except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n// Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n// not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n// Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n// DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n//\n// The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n// an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n// If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n// replace events for backwards compatibility. Default informers do not pass\n// existing objects to Replace though.\n//\n// Note that TransformFunc is called while inserting objects into the\n// notification queue and is therefore extremely performance sensitive; please\n// do not do anything that will take a long time.\ntype TransformFunc func(interface{}) (interface{}, error)\n\n// DeltaType is the type of a change (addition, deletion, etc)\ntype DeltaType string\n\n// Change type definition\nconst (\n\tAdded   DeltaType = \"Added\"\n\tUpdated DeltaType = \"Updated\"\n\tDeleted DeltaType = \"Deleted\"\n\t// Replaced is emitted when we encountered watch errors and had to do a\n\t// relist, or on initial listing of objects. We don't know if the replaced\n\t// object has changed.\n\t//\n\t// NOTE: Previous versions of DeltaFIFO would use Sync for Replace events\n\t// as well. This is the same reason as\n\t// Replaced but will be emitted instead when the FIFO supports atomic\n\t// replacement. This event will return the full list of replaced items\n\t// instead of a single object.\n\tReplacedAll DeltaType = \"ReplacedAll\"\n\t// Sync is for synthetic events during a periodic resync.\n\tSync DeltaType = \"Sync\"\n\t// SyncAll indicates all known objects should be reprocessed.\n\t// This event contains an object of type SyncAllInfo.\n\tSyncAll DeltaType = \"SyncAll\"\n)\n\n// Delta is a member of Deltas (a list of Delta objects) which\n// in its turn is the type stored by a DeltaFIFO. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_015zV7KhRzXb6rPm5jATL77J","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01XfH6B7YqXFbWt3QxMkM7Z3","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/workspace/documentation.md","content":"# Client-go: Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## Overview\n\n**client-go** is the official Go client library for interacting with Kubernetes clusters. It provides a comprehensive toolkit for API access, event-driven caching, and controller implementation. client-go serves as the foundation for kubectl, controller-manager, and nearly all custom controllers running in production Kubernetes environments.\n\nThis guide explains the internal architecture, component responsibilities, request flow, and extension mechanisms that enable client-go to efficiently and reliably mediate between Go applications and Kubernetes API servers at scale.\n\n---\n\n## Subsystem Boundaries and Dependencies\n\n### Purpose and Scope\n\nclient-go abstracts the complexity of Kubernetes API communication into layered interfaces, each solving specific problems:\n\n1. **Configuration Management** - Loading and merging kubeconfig files, handling authentication providers\n2. **Network Transport** - HTTP/2 communication with TLS, authentication headers, rate limiting, and retry logic\n3. **API Interaction** - Type-safe (typed clients) and type-agnostic (dynamic client) interfaces for CRUD operations\n4. **Event-Driven Caching** - Efficient, event-sourced cache population using LIST/WATCH primitives\n5. **Controller Infrastructure** - Patterns for building stateful, reliable controllers with exponential backoff and leadership semantics\n\n### Upstream Dependencies\n\n- **k8s.io/apimachinery** - Core types, runtime serialization, watch primitives, and error definitions\n- **k8s.io/api** - Generated API resource types (Pod, Deployment, etc.)\n- **Go standard library** - net/http, crypto/tls, context, sync primitives\n- **golang.org/x/net/http2** - HTTP/2 protocol support\n- **k8s.io/utils** - Utility functions for exponential backoff, clock mocking\n\n### Downstream Consumers\n\n- **kubectl** - CLI tool for cluster interaction (uses clientcmd and typed clients)\n- **kube-controller-manager** - In-cluster controllers (uses informers and listers)\n- **kubelet** - Node agent (uses dynamic client and informers)\n- **Custom controllers** - User-defined operators and webhooks (use full client-go stack)\n- **API Testing Tools** - Integration tests and fake clients\n\n---\n\n## Core Architecture and Components\n\n### Layer 1: Configuration and Client Initialization\n\n#### Rest.Config: The Configuration Object\n\nThe `rest.Config` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go`) is an in-memory representation of all settings needed to communicate with an API server:\n\n```go\ntype Config struct {\n\tHost string                    // API server address\n\tAPIPath string                 // e.g., \"/api\"\n\tContentConfig                  // Serialization format (JSON, Protobuf, CBOR)\n\tBearerToken string             // Authentication token\n\tBearerTokenFile string         // File to reload token from\n\tUsername string                // Basic auth username\n\tTLSClientConfig                // Client certificate/key paths\n\tQPS float32                    // Requests per second (default: 5)\n\tBurst int                      // Burst requests (default: 10)\n\tRateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n\tTimeout time.Duration          // Request timeout\n\tTransport http.RoundTripper    // Custom HTTP transport\n\tWrapTransport transport.WrapperFunc\n\tWarningHandler WarningHandler   // Processes server warnings\n}\n```\n\n**Design principle**: Configuration is separate from usage. This allows loading configuration once and reusing it across multiple clients.\n\n#### Tools/Clientcmd: Configuration Loading\n\nThe `clientcmd` package (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/clientcmd/`) handles kubeconfig file parsing, context selection, and auth provider instantiation:\n\nKey components:\n- **ClientConfigLoader** (`loader.go`) - Reads YAML kubeconfig files\n- **ClientConfig** (`client_config.go`) - Builds `rest.Config` from kubeconfig\n- **AuthLoaders** (`auth_loaders.go`) - Handles authentication plugins (OIDC, Exec, GCP, Azure)\n- **BuildConfigFromFlags()** - High-level entry point that handles both in-cluster and kubeconfig paths\n\nFlow:\n```\nkubeconfig file ‚Üí Merge contexts ‚Üí Select context ‚Üí AuthProvider.Login() ‚Üí rest.Config\n```\n\n#### InCluster vs OutOfCluster Configuration\n\n**In-Cluster** (`rest.InClusterConfig()`):\n- Reads service account token from `/var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/token`\n- Derives API server host from `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_HOST` and `KUBERNETES_SERVICE_PORT` environment variables\n- Uses default certificate chain from `/var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/ca.crt`\n\n**Out-of-Cluster** (`clientcmd.BuildConfigFromFlags()`):\n- Reads kubeconfig (default: `~/.kube/config`)\n- Supports kubectl context switching and user selection\n- Delegates to auth providers for token refresh (OIDC, exec-based providers)\n\n### Layer 2: REST Client and Transport\n\n#### RESTClient: The HTTP Foundation\n\nThe `RESTClient` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/client.go`) is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all higher-level interfaces. It implements a builder pattern for request construction:\n\n```go\ntype RESTClient struct {\n\tbase *url.URL                         // Root API URL\n\tversionedAPIPath string               // Group/version path segment\n\tcontent requestClientContentConfigProvider\n\tcreateBackoffMgr func() BackoffManagerWithContext\n\trateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n\twarningHandler WarningHandlerWithContext\n\tClient *http.Client\n}\n\n// Builder pattern example usage:\nclient.Post().\n\tResource(\"pods\").\n\tNamespace(\"default\").\n\tBody(podObject).\n\tInto(&result)\n```\n\n**Key design decisions**:\n1. **Deferred execution** - Methods like `.Get()`, `.Post()` return a `Request` object; actual HTTP calls happen at `.Into()` or `.DoRaw()`\n2. **Content negotiation** - Supports multiple wire formats (JSON, Protobuf, CBOR via feature gate)\n3. **Subresource handling** - Can target `/status`, `/scale`, `/logs`, `/exec` via `.SubResource()`\n\n#### Request Building and Execution\n\nA `Request` object (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go`) builds HTTP requests and handles response decoding:\n\n```go\ntype Request struct {\n\tc *RESTClient\n\tverb string              // GET, POST, PUT, PATCH, DELETE\n\tpathPrefix string        // /api or /apis\n\tsubresource string       // status, scale, logs\n\tnamespace string\n\tresource string          // pods, deployments\n\tname string              // specific object name\n\tparams url.Values        // Query parameters\n\tbody interface{}         // Object to send\n\tbackoff wait.BackoffManager\n\ttimeout time.Duration\n}\n```\n\n**Request execution flow**:\n1. `.Verb()` + `.Resource()` + `.Name()` build the URL\n2. `.Body()` serializes the object using negotiated codec\n3. Content-Type and Accept headers are set via `ClientContentConfig.Negotiator`\n4. `.Into(&result)` executes the request and decodes response\n5. On non-2xx status, response is deserialized into `errors.StatusError` for programmatic handling\n\n#### HTTP Transport Layer\n\nThe transport chain (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/transport/`) implements RoundTripper middleware:\n\n```\nUser Request\n    ‚Üì\nWarningHandler (processes Warning headers from server)\n    ‚Üì\nRateLimiter (client-side flow control)\n    ‚Üì\nBackoff Retry (exponential backoff on failures)\n    ‚Üì\nBearerToken Injection\n    ‚Üì\nImpersonation Headers (User/Groups/UID for delegation)\n    ‚Üì\nCertificate Rotation (in-flight cert refresh for kubelets)\n    ‚Üì\nTLS/mTLS Layer\n    ‚Üì\nHTTP/2 Transport\n    ‚Üì\nAPI Server\n```\n\nKey transport components:\n- **round_trippers.go** - RoundTripper wrappers for auth, rate limiting, retries\n- **token_source.go** - OAuth2-like token refresh for OIDC providers\n- **cert_rotation.go** - Reloads client certificates without connection restart\n\n#### Rate Limiting and Backoff\n\nClient-side rate limiting is dual-layer:\n\n1. **Token Bucket Rate Limiter** (`QPS` and `Burst` in rest.Config)\n   - Default: 5 QPS, 10 Burst\n   - Prevents overwhelming API server\n   - Configurable via `config.QPS` and `config.Burst`\n\n2. **Server-Side 429 Handling** (automatic in round_trippers.go)\n   - Reads `Retry-After` header from 429 responses\n   - Implements exponential backoff\n   - Integrates with client-side rate limiter\n\n**Design rationale**: Client-side limiting is coarse-grained (all operations); server-side limiting via API Priority and Fairness is fine-grained (per-priority-level). Both are necessary for cluster stability.\n\n### Layer 3: Typed and Dynamic Clients\n\n#### Kubernetes.Clientset: Type-Safe Interface\n\nThe `kubernetes.Clientset` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/kubernetes/`) provides strongly-typed, generated interfaces for all core and built-in APIs:\n\n```go\ntype Clientset struct {\n\tadmissionregistration *admissionregistrationv1.AdmissionRegistrationV1Client\n\tapps *appsv1.AppsV1Client\n\tbatch *batchv1.BatchV1Client\n\tcore *corev1.CoreV1Client\n\t// ... one client per API group/version\n}\n\n// Usage example:\npods, err := clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").List(ctx, metav1.ListOptions{})\n```\n\n**Code generation**: The typed clients are generated from API types using `k8s.io/code-generator`. The generator produces:\n- Interface definitions matching resource operations (List, Get, Create, Update, Delete, etc.)\n- Concrete implementations wrapping RESTClient\n- Verb-based request builders\n\n**Type safety benefits**:\n- Compile-time validation of API group/version availability\n- IDE autocomplete and refactoring support\n- Prevents string-based typos in resource names\n\n#### Dynamic Client: Untyped, Extensible\n\nThe `dynamic.DynamicClient` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/dynamic/`) represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`:\n\n```go\ntype DynamicClient struct {\n\t// Internally uses RESTClient for each GroupVersionResource\n}\n\n// Usage: works with any resource, including CRDs\ngvr := schema.GroupVersionResource{\n\tGroup: \"custom.io\", Version: \"v1\", Resource: \"widgets\"\n}\nresult, err := dynamicClient.Resource(gvr).Namespace(\"default\").\n\tList(ctx, metav1.ListOptions{})\n```\n\n**DynamicClient enables**:\n- CRD support without code generation\n- Generic tooling (kubectl, kube-apiserver, metrics-server)\n- Runtime discovery of cluster capabilities\n\n**Limitation**: No schema validation before sending to server; schema awareness comes from optional OpenAPI schema fetching.\n\n#### Discovery Client: Schema and Capability Discovery\n\nThe `discovery.DiscoveryClient` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/discovery/`) determines what resources exist and their capabilities:\n\n```go\n// Discover API resources\napis, err := discoveryClient.ServerGroups()\n// Discover within a group/version\nresources, err := discoveryClient.ServerResourcesForGroupVersion(\"v1\")\n// Fetch OpenAPI v3 schema\nspec, err := discoveryClient.OpenAPIV3()\n```\n\n**Caching optimization**: `discovery.CachedDiscoveryClient` (`cached/`) caches discovery results on disk to reduce startup time and API server load. Cache invalidates on version mismatch or manual refresh.\n\n### Layer 4: Informers and Caching\n\n#### The Informer Pattern: Event-Driven State\n\nThe informer mechanism (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/informers/` and `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/`) replaces synchronous polling with event-driven caching. This is essential for scalable controllers.\n\n**Problem solved**: Controllers need current state of many objects. Polling all objects continuously is expensive. Watching all objects consumes server resources. The solution: LIST once, WATCH incrementally, process events.\n\n#### Reflector: LIST/WATCH Orchestrator\n\nThe `Reflector` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/reflector.go`) is the data pump:\n\n```go\ntype Reflector struct {\n\tname string\n\tstore ReflectorStore           // Where to push changes\n\tlisterWatcher ListerWatcherWithContext\n\tlastSyncResourceVersion string // RV from last successful LIST\n\tresyncPeriod time.Duration     // How often to re-list everything\n\tminWatchTimeout time.Duration  // Random 5-10 min for load distribution\n\tShouldResync func() bool       // Periodic resync trigger\n}\n\n// ListAndWatch loop (simplified):\n// 1. LIST to get initial RV\n// 2. WATCH from that RV\n// 3. On \"too old\" error, re-LIST and restart WATCH\n// 4. Periodically call Resync() to catch missed deletes\n```\n\n**Resource Version**: A server-side opaque token indicating a point in time. Essential for consistency:\n- `LIST` with empty RV: latest (but potentially inconsistent with following WATCH)\n- `WATCH` with specific RV: stream changes after that RV\n- \"too old\" error: RV expired from server's watch cache (default 5 min); must re-list\n\n**Watch Bookmarks optimization**: Servers send periodic bookmark events with updated RV, allowing Reflector to restart from a recent point without re-listing.\n\n**Pagination via WatchList**: The `useWatchList` flag (`reflector.go` line 147) enables streaming paginated results instead of chunked LIST calls. Advantages:\n- Fewer server memory spikes\n- Lower network latency (streaming vs buffered)\n\n#### DeltaFIFO: Event Accumulation Queue\n\nThe `DeltaFIFO` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/delta_fifo.go`) is a producer-consumer queue that accumulates changes:\n\n```go\n// Delta types\nconst (\n\tAdded     DeltaType = \"Added\"\n\tUpdated   DeltaType = \"Updated\"\n\tDeleted   DeltaType = \"Deleted\"\n\tReplaced  DeltaType = \"Replaced\"    // re-list happened\n\tSync      DeltaType = \"Sync\"        // periodic resync\n\tSyncAll   DeltaType = \"SyncAll\"     // bulk resync\n\tReplacedAll DeltaType = \"ReplacedAll\" // bulk with atomicity\n)\n\ntype DeltaFIFO struct {\n\tlock sync.RWMutex\n\tcond sync.Cond\n\titems map[string]Deltas         // Key -> [Delta, Delta, ...]\n\tqueue []string                  // FIFO key ordering\n\tpopulated bool                  // First Replace() done?\n\tknownObjects KeyListerGetter    // Reference set for detecting deletes\n}\n```\n\n**Key insight**: A `Deltas` is a slice of changes for one object. When Pop() is called, all accumulated changes since the last Pop() are returned together. This solves the problem of rapid changes between processing cycles:\n\n```\nWatch events: Pod-A Added, Pod-A Updated, Pod-A Updated\nDeltaFIFO stores: Pod-A -> [Added, Updated, Updated]\nPop() returns: (Pod-A, [Added, Updated, Updated])\nController processes all three deltas and sees final state correctly\n```\n\n**Delete detection via KnownObjects**: When `Replace()` is called (full re-list), DeltaFIFO compares the new list against `knownObjects` (from Indexer). Objects in `knownObjects` but not in new list are emitted as Deleted deltas, catching deletions missed by watch.\n\n#### Indexer and SharedIndexInformer: The Cache and Processor\n\nThe `Indexer` (part of `cache.Store` interface) is the actual in-memory cache:\n\n```go\ntype Store interface {\n\tAdd(obj interface{}) error\n\tUpdate(obj interface{}) error\n\tDelete(obj interface{}) error\n\tList() []interface{}\n\tGet(obj interface{}) (interface{}, exists bool, err error)\n\tGetByKey(key string) (interface{}, exists bool, err error)\n}\n```\n\nThe `SharedIndexInformer` (`shared_index_informer.go`) coordinates everything:\n\n```go\ntype sharedIndexInformer struct {\n\tindexer Indexer                    // The cache store\n\tprocessor *sharedProcessor\n\tlisterWatcher ListerWatcherWithContext\n\tobjectType runtime.Object\n\tresyncCheckPeriod time.Duration\n\tstarted bool\n\tcontroller Controller               // Runs Reflector + DeltaFIFO\n\treflector *Reflector\n}\n\n// Start() launches:\n// 1. controller.Run() ‚Üí Reflector.ListAndWatch()\n// 2. processor.run() ‚Üí Event handler dispatch\n```\n\n**SharedIndexInformer responsibilities**:\n1. **Caching**: Indexer stores objects with multiple query indexes (by namespace, by owner, custom)\n2. **Watching**: Reflector maintains live sync via LIST/WATCH\n3. **Event dispatch**: Calls registered AddFunc, UpdateFunc, DeleteFunc handlers\n4. **Work isolation**: Handlers only add keys to workqueue; actual reconciliation is separate\n\n**Thread safety**: Indexer is thread-safe for reads (Listers can query without locks); updates come through controlled channels from processor.\n\n#### Listers: Read-Only Cache Access\n\nTyped Listers (generated, e.g., `corev1.PodLister`) provide read-only, indexed access:\n\n```go\ntype PodLister interface {\n\tList(selector labels.Selector) ([]*v1.Pod, error)\n\tPods(namespace string) PodNamespaceLister\n}\n\ntype PodNamespaceLister interface {\n\tList(selector labels.Selector) ([]*v1.Pod, error)\n\tGet(name string) (*v1.Pod, error)\n}\n```\n\n**Why Listers over Indexer?**:\n- Enforce read-only contract (no accidental mutations)\n- Provide type safety (return concrete `*Pod`, not `interface{}`)\n- Generated with custom indexes for common query patterns\n- Lightweight wrapper around Indexer for zero-copy access\n\n### Layer 5: Controller Patterns and Utilities\n\n#### WorkQueue: Buffering and Retry\n\nThe `util/workqueue` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/`) decouples event detection from reconciliation:\n\n```go\ntype RateLimitingInterface interface {\n\tAdd(item interface{})                    // Enqueue item\n\tGet() (item interface{}, shutdown bool)  // Dequeue and block\n\tDone(item interface{})                   // Mark done\n\tAddRateLimited(item interface{})         // Enqueue with backoff\n\tForget(item interface{})                 // Clear failure count\n\tNumRequeues(item interface{}) int        // How many times failed?\n}\n```\n\n**Rate limiting strategies**:\n- `RateLimitingInterface` with pluggable `RateLimiter`\n- `DefaultControllerRateLimiter()`: Exponential backoff (1s ‚Üí 2s ‚Üí 4s ‚Üí ... up to 15min)\n- Prevents hammering API server on transient errors\n- Allows long retry windows for permanent-seeming errors\n\n**Pattern**:\n```go\n// Informer handler: just enqueue the key\nfunc(obj interface{}) {\n\tkey, _ := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n\tworkqueue.AddRateLimited(key)\n}\n\n// Controller loop: dequeue and process\nfor {\n\tkey, _ := workqueue.Get()\n\tif err := controller.process(key) != nil {\n\t\tworkqueue.AddRateLimited(key) // Retry\n\t} else {\n\t\tworkqueue.Forget(key)          // Success\n\t}\n\tworkqueue.Done(key)\n}\n```\n\n#### LeaderElection: Single-Writer Semantics\n\nThe `tools/leaderelection` (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/`) provides distributed leadership via Lease objects:\n\n```go\ntype LeaderElectionConfig struct {\n\tLock resourcelock.Interface    // Lease, ConfigMap, or other lock\n\tLeaseDuration time.Duration   // How long before lease expires\n\tRenewDeadline time.Duration   // Must renew before this\n\tRetryPeriod time.Duration     // How often to try acquiring lock\n\tCallbacks LeaderCallbacks      // OnStartedLeading, OnStoppedLeading\n}\n\n// High-level: runs a loop acquiring/renewing the lock\nleaderelection.RunOrDie(ctx, config)\n```\n\n**Implementation**: Competing replicas write to a shared `Lease` object. The replica whose write succeeds and periodically refreshes is the leader. Others watch the Lease and take over on timeout.\n\n**Design advantage**: Uses existing Kubernetes API for consensus, no external system needed (unlike etcd consensus).\n\n### Layer 6: Server-Side Apply\n\nServer-Side Apply (`applyconfigurations/` and `util/apply/`) is an alternative to get-modify-update:\n\n```go\n// Type-safe builders (generated)\npod := corev1ac.Pod(\"my-pod\", \"default\").\n\tWithSpec(corev1ac.PodSpec().\n\t\tWithContainers(corev1ac.Container().\n\t\t\tWithName(\"app\").\n\t\t\tWithImage(\"image:v1\")))\n\n// Server handles merge conflicts\nerr := clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").\n\tApply(ctx, pod, metav1.ApplyOptions{FieldManager: \"my-app\"})\n```\n\n**Key advantages over get-modify-update**:\n- Multiple controllers can co-manage the same object safely\n- Server resolves conflicts based on field ownership\n- No need for optimistic locking (conflict checking)\n- Idempotent by design\n\n---\n\n## End-to-End Request Flow\n\n### Scenario: Controller Processing a Pod Change\n\n```\n1. Reflector observes Pod update on API server\n   ‚îî‚îÄ GET /api/v1/pods?watch=true&resourceVersion=12345\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Receives MODIFIED event with updated Pod object\n\n2. Reflector adds Pod to DeltaFIFO\n   ‚îî‚îÄ DeltaFIFO.Add(pod) creates/appends Delta(Updated, pod)\n\n3. SharedIndexInformer.controller.process() pops DeltaFIFO\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Receives Deltas for pod (may include prior Add/Update)\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Updates Indexer with final state\n\n4. Indexer triggers event handlers\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Calls registered AddFunc/UpdateFunc (from eventHandler)\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Handler extracts pod.Name and adds to workqueue\n\n5. Controller loop dequeues work\n   ‚îî‚îÄ workqueue.Get() returns \"default/my-pod\"\n\n6. Controller.Reconcile(ctx, \"default/my-pod\")\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Fetches fresh Pod from Lister (from Indexer cache)\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Computes desired state based on business logic\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Calls clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Update(ctx, newPod)\n\n7. RESTClient builds and sends HTTP request\n   ‚îî‚îÄ PUT /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/my-pod HTTP/1.1\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Auth headers, TLS, rate limit, backoff middleware applied\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Request sent over HTTP/2\n\n8. API server responds with updated Pod\n\n9. RESTClient decodes response into Pod object\n   ‚îî‚îÄ Returned to controller\n\n10. Controller checks result; if error, calls workqueue.AddRateLimited()\n    ‚îî‚îÄ Item retried with exponential backoff\n    ‚îî‚îÄ On success, workqueue.Forget() clears retry count\n\n11. Reflector detects the update via WATCH (or next LIST)\n    ‚îî‚îÄ Loop back to step 1\n```\n\n### Typed vs. Dynamic Client Request Flow Differences\n\n**Typed Client (e.g., `clientset.CoreV1().Pods().List()`)**:\n```\nConfig ‚Üí RESTClient ‚Üí Request.Group(\"\").Version(\"v1\").Resource(\"pods\")\n‚Üí \".Into(&corev1.PodList)\" ‚Üí Deserialized concrete *corev1.PodList\n```\n\n**Dynamic Client (e.g., `dynamicClient.Resource(gvr).List()`)**:\n```\nConfig ‚Üí RESTClient ‚Üí Request.Group(\"custom.io\").Version(\"v1\").Resource(\"widgets\")\n‚Üí \".Into(&unstructured.UnstructuredList)\" ‚Üí Generic object with []map[string]interface{}\n```\n\nBoth use the same RESTClient transport; difference is only in response deserialization (codec).\n\n---\n\n## Failure Modes and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### Watch Timeout and Reconnection\n\n**Problem**: Long-lived watch streams can be terminated by proxies, firewalls, or load balancers after a fixed time.\n\n**Solution** (in Reflector):\n- Randomized minWatchTimeout (default 5-10 minutes)\n- When timeout occurs, Reflector detects connection drop\n- Calls WatchErrorHandler to log the error\n- Falls back to a new LIST call to get fresh resourceVersion\n- Resumes watch from new resourceVersion\n\n**Tradeoff**: Periodic LIST calls consume slightly more API server CPU than pure watch, but provide resilience. The algorithm balances load distribution via random timeouts.\n\n### Resource Version Expiration (\"too old\")\n\n**Problem**: Watch cache on API server expires old resourceVersions (typically after 5 minutes). If a controller is slow to catch up, it may request a watch from an expired RV.\n\n**Solution** (in Reflector):\n```go\ncase isExpiredError(err):\n    r.setLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable()\n    // Causes next ListAndWatch to LIST from scratch\n```\n\n**Cost**: Re-listing is more expensive than resuming from RV, but necessary for correctness. Prevents a controller from silently missing events.\n\n**Watch Bookmarks optimization**: Server sends periodic BOOKMARK events with current RV, allowing Reflector to fast-forward without re-listing.\n\n### Eventual Consistency of Indexer\n\n**Problem**: Events arrive out of order, or an event is dropped. Indexer could diverge from API server state.\n\n**Solution** (via periodic Resync):\n- Reflector periodically calls DeltaFIFO.Resync()\n- Resync() emits Sync deltas for all objects in Indexer\n- Controller reprocesses all objects, achieving eventual consistency\n- Default resync period: 0 (disabled; custom controllers set as needed)\n\n**Tradeoff**: Resync increases API server load (triggers Lister calls even without changes). Many production controllers disable resync and rely on watch accuracy.\n\n### Watch List Pagination (new in Kubernetes 1.27)\n\n**Problem**: Large LIST responses can spike memory on API server. Older pattern: chunk the LIST, then open a watch (unpaginatedresult) or stream the watch (paginatedresult).\n\n**Solution**: The `WatchList` feature streams paginated results in a single watch-like connection.\n\n**Tradeoff**: Requires server support (Kubernetes 1.27+). Falls back to chunked LIST if server doesn't support. Operators must explicitly enable `reflector.useWatchList = true`.\n\n### Shared Indexer Concurrency\n\n**Problem**: Informer handlers run concurrently; multiple controllers may query Indexer simultaneously while it's being updated.\n\n**Solution**:\n- Indexer uses read-write locks for mutations\n- DeltaFIFO and processor use locks to serialize updates\n- Listers perform snapshot reads (acquire read lock, read, release)\n- Controllers see occasional stale reads, but never corrupted data\n\n**Limit**: Highly concurrent workloads (100s of controllers) may contend on the Indexer lock. Mitigation: each controller gets its own SharedInformerFactory, distributing lock contention.\n\n### Client-Side Rate Limiting vs. Server-Side APF\n\n**Tradeoff**:\n- Client-side (`QPS`, `Burst`) is coarse-grained: applies to all operations equally\n- Server-side (API Priority and Fairness) is fine-grained: per-priority-level, per-user, per-namespace\n\n**Design**: Both layers are necessary. Client-side prevents a single client from overwhelming the server. Server-side ensures fair allocation among multiple clients. They work together:\n```\nClient A respects QPS=5 ‚îÄ‚îê\nClient B respects QPS=5 ‚îú‚îÄ‚Üí API Server APF system\nClient C respects QPS=5 ‚îÄ‚îò\n                        ‚Üì\n             APF allocates seats fairly across high/medium/low priority\n```\n\n### Memory Footprint of Caching\n\n**Problem**: Large clusters have many objects. Storing all Pods, Nodes, Secrets in-memory is expensive.\n\n**Mitigations**:\n1. **TransformFunc** (`/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/`):\n   - Strip sensitive fields before caching (e.g., remove large Secret data)\n   - Reduce memory by dropping unnecessary fields\n   - Idempotent; safe to apply to objects during Replace()\n\n2. **Namespace filtering** (WithNamespace option in SharedInformerFactory):\n   - Only cache objects in specific namespace(s)\n   - Common pattern: each controller watches only its namespace\n\n3. **Field selection** (TweakListOptions):\n   - Server can filter fields in List response\n   - Further reduces network and memory\n\n---\n\n## Extension Points and Customization\n\n### Custom RoundTrippers\n\nInject middleware into the HTTP transport chain:\n\n```go\nconfig := rest.Config{...}\nconfig.WrapTransport = func(rt http.RoundTripper) http.RoundTripper {\n\treturn &CustomLoggingRoundTripper{rt: rt}\n}\n```\n\n**Use cases**:\n- Request/response logging (with sanitization)\n- Custom retry logic (e.g., jitter, circuit breakers)\n- Observability (distributed tracing, metrics)\n- Chaos injection for testing\n\n### Custom Rate Limiters\n\nReplace default token-bucket with custom backoff logic:\n\n```go\ntype CustomRateLimiter struct { ... }\nfunc (c *CustomRateLimiter) TryAccept() bool { ... }\n\nconfig.RateLimiter = CustomRateLimiter{...}\n```\n\n**Use cases**:\n- Exponential jitter (prevents thundering herd on server recovery)\n- Context-aware limiting (different rates based on priority)\n- Adaptive limiting (feedback loop from server response times)\n\n### Custom Indexers\n\nAdd secondary indexes for efficient queries:\n\n```go\nindexers := cache.Indexers{\n\t\"controllerRef\": func(obj interface{}) ([]string, error) {\n\t\tpod := obj.(*v1.Pod)\n\t\tif owner := metav1.GetControllerOf(pod); owner != nil {\n\t\t\treturn []string{fmt.Sprintf(\"%s/%s\", owner.Kind, owner.Name)}, nil\n\t\t}\n\t\treturn []string{}, nil\n\t},\n}\nindexer := cache.NewIndexer(cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc, indexers)\n```\n\n**Use cases**:\n- Find all Pods owned by a Deployment (via controllerRef)\n- Find all Endpoints backing a Service\n- Custom query patterns for domain logic\n\n**Risk**: Custom indexers must be deterministic and idempotent. Bugs can leave cache in inconsistent state.\n\n### Event Handlers and Reconciliation Hooks\n\nAttach custom handlers to SharedInformerFactory:\n\n```go\ninformer.AddEventHandler(cache.ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n\tAddFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n\t\tkey, _ := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n\t\tc.workqueue.Add(key)\n\t},\n\tUpdateFunc: func(oldObj, newObj interface{}) {\n\t\tkey, _ := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(newObj)\n\t\tc.workqueue.Add(key)\n\t},\n\tDeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n\t\tkey, _ := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n\t\tc.workqueue.Add(key)\n\t},\n})\n```\n\n**Design principle**: Keep handlers simple. They run synchronously in the processor loop. Expensive logic goes in the controller reconciliation loop (separate goroutines).\n\n**Risk**: Slow handlers block processing of subsequent events. If a handler panics, it halts the processor. Use `defer` to recover, or consider structured error handling.\n\n### Custom Authentication Providers\n\nImplement `clientcmdapi.AuthProvider`:\n\n```go\ntype CustomAuthProvider struct { ... }\nfunc (c *CustomAuthProvider) Login() (*rest.Config, error) { ... }\n```\n\nRegister in kubeconfig authProvider section:\n```yaml\nusers:\n- name: my-user\n  user:\n    exec:\n      command: /path/to/custom-auth-plugin\n      args: [...]\n```\n\n**Use cases**:\n- OIDC flow custom handling\n- Hardware token integration (e.g., YubiKey)\n- Cloud-provider-specific auth (AWS IAM, GCP Workload Identity)\n\n**Risk**: Auth plugins run during config loading. Slow plugins delay client initialization. Credentials cached in memory; ensure no logging leaks them.\n\n### Custom TransformFunc\n\nTransform objects before caching:\n\n```go\nconfig := rest.InClusterConfig()\nconfig.TransformFunc = func(obj interface{}) (interface{}, error) {\n\tsecret := obj.(*v1.Secret)\n\t// Zero out sensitive fields\n\tsecret.Data = nil\n\treturn secret, nil\n}\n```\n\n**Use cases**:\n- Strip large fields (e.g., Secret data, ConfigMap values)\n- Normalize objects for memory efficiency\n- Audit/logging fields before cache\n\n**Constraints**:\n- Must be idempotent (applied to objects during Replace)\n- Must not break object structure (controllers still need metadata)\n- Performance-critical: called for every object during initial list\n\n---\n\n## Operational Implications\n\n### Performance Considerations\n\n1. **Informer startup time**: First LIST call must fetch all objects of a type. Large clusters (10k+ Pods) may take 10-30 seconds. Mitigate with:\n   - Namespace filtering\n   - Field selection (TweakListOptions)\n   - Parallel informers (separate factories per API group)\n\n2. **Watch reconnection storms**: If API server is under load, many controllers trying to reconnect simultaneously can amplify load. Mitigations:\n   - Randomized minWatchTimeout spreads reconnections\n   - Exponential backoff in WatchErrorHandler\n   - Monitor \"reflector_list_duration_seconds\" metric\n\n3. **Indexer contention**: High-volume event streams (e.g., logging from all Pods) can saturate Indexer lock. Mitigations:\n   - Multiple SharedInformerFactory instances (per logical component)\n   - Reduce event volume via label/field selectors\n   - Monitor \"workqueue_depth\" and handler latencies\n\n4. **Memory scaling**: Cache size scales with cluster size. For a 5000-node cluster:\n   - Typical node object: ~50KB\n   - 5000 nodes √ó 50KB = ~250MB per informer\n   - Plus Pods, Endpoints, Services, etc.\n   - Typical controller footprint: 500MB‚Äì2GB\n\n### Reliability and Failure Handling\n\n1. **API server outage**: Informer watch drops. Reflector retries with exponential backoff, periodically attempting LIST. Controller work queues local items. Once API server recovers, catch-up happens via Resync or watch re-establishment.\n\n2. **Network partition**: Long-lived watch connections timeout (minWatchTimeout). Reflector detects and reconnects. No events are lost if watch reconnects within resourceVersion TTL (default 5 min).\n\n3. **OOM on client**: If Indexer grows too large, process may OOM. No in-built circuit breaker. Mitigations:\n   - TransformFunc to reduce object size\n   - Namespace filtering\n   - External monitoring to alert on memory growth\n   - Increase memory limits for critical controllers\n\n4. **Slow reconciliation loop**: If controller.Reconcile() is slow, work queue backlog grows. Informer keeps pushing events. Risk of memory explosion if queue is unbounded. Mitigation: implement backpressure (e.g., wait for queue depth before starting more handlers).\n\n### Debugging and Observability\n\n**Metrics** (`tools/metrics/`):\n- `workqueue_adds_total` - Total items added to queue\n- `workqueue_depth` - Current queue depth\n- `reflector_items_per_list_total` - How many items per LIST\n- `reflector_list_duration_seconds` - Time to LIST\n- `reflector_watch_duration_seconds` - How long watch lasts before reconnect\n\n**Logging**:\n- Set klog verbosity: `-v=2` (watch errors), `-v=4` (watch events), `-v=6` (detailed request info)\n- Reflector logs at `info` level for drops, at `debug` level for operations\n- Processor logs slow handlers and panics\n\n**Tracing**:\n- RESTClient can emit OpenTelemetry spans if instrumented\n- Roundtrip latencies visible in metrics\n\n**Local testing**:\n- `fake.Clientset()` - In-memory test client\n- `fake.NewSimpleDynamicClient()` - Fake dynamic client\n- `cache.NewIndexer()` with in-memory store for testing informer logic\n\n---\n\n## Key Files and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Purpose |\n|------|---------|\n| `/workspace/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/` | HTTP client, Principles\n\n1. **Layered abstraction**: Configuration ‚Üí Transport ‚Üí REST Client ‚Üí Typed/Dynamic Clients. Each layer has clear responsibilities, allowing composition and customization.\n\n2. **Event-driven over polling**: Informers and Reflectors replace continuous polling with efficient LIST/WATCH, reducing API server load and improving responsiveness.\n\n3. **Eventual consistency**: Periodic Resync ensures Indexer eventually converges with API server state, even if watch events are dropped or misordered.\n\n4. **Type safety via code generation**: Typed clients reduce errors and improve IDE support. DynamicClient remains available for CRDs and generic tools.\n\n5. **Decoupled event detection and reconciliation**: WorkQueue isolates watch/handler processing from business logic, enabling retries and backoff without blocking event stream.\n\n6. **Robustness through resilience**: Exponential backoff, timeout recovery, and eventual consistency provide automatic recovery from transient failures without manual intervention.\n\n7. **Extensibility at multiple levels**: Custom RoundTrippers, rate limiters, indexers, auth providers, and handlers enable adapting client-go to specific operational needs without forking.\n\nThese principles allow client-go to serve as the foundation for Kubernetes control plane components and user controllers alike, handling millions of objects and thousands of concurrent operations reliably.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":2786,"cache_creation_input_tokens":295,"cache_read_input_tokens":54101,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":295},"output_tokens":2,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"411906ed-b92f-4f45-933d-d82314af31ab","uuid":"68c8ff73-dd2e-4366-af05-4f3a039ae065"}
